DISPUTE RESOLUTION

Students "will be immediately admitted to the school in which enrollment is sought, pending resolution" of disputes. Parents/guardians receive written determination notices with appeal rights to the district and ultimately to Colorado's state coordinator.

TRANSPORTATION PROVISIONS

Within-district transportation is arranged through coordinator collaboration with the transportation director when requested. Between-district placements require both districts to either agree on cost-sharing methods or split costs equally.

CONFIDENTIALITY AND NON-DISCRIMINATION

Housing situation information is "treated as a confidential student education record" and not directory information. Employees cannot discriminate against eligible students, and violations may result in discipline "up to and including dismissal from employment."

ATTENDANCE PROTECTIONS

The district removes attendance barriers collaboratively. "No student under McKinney-Vento status will be withdrawn from a school until the student has enrolled in a new school or without approval from the McKinney-Vento coordinator."

No mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools was found in this policy.

## JFABD-R — Homeless Students (Regulation)

HOMELESS STUDENT LIAISON

The superintendent appoints a liaison to "identify homeless children and youth and to facilitate their access to and success in school." Key functions include mediating school selection disputes, arranging transportation, requesting records, providing service referrals, and assisting unaccompanied youth with enrollment.

ENROLLMENT

Homeless students receive "immediate" enrollment regardless of missing standard documentation. When immunizations or medical records are needed, "the school shall immediately refer the parent/guardian or unaccompanied youth to the homeless student liaison" for assistance obtaining these items.

ENROLLMENT DETERMINATION

Students may attend their school of origin throughout homelessness or enroll in their current residence attendance area. When disputes arise, the liaison consults with parents/guardians, principals, transportation directors, and liaisons from other districts.

Decision factors considered include:
- Student's best interests
- Keeping students in original school when feasible
- Student and parent/guardian preferences
- School's ability to meet educational needs
- Transportation considerations

ENROLLMENT DISPUTES

Students "shall be immediately admitted to the school in which enrollment is sought, pending resolution of the dispute." Written notice of determinations and appeal rights goes to parents/guardians. Appeals proceed through the assistant superintendent, then to the Colorado Department of Education's homeless coordinator.

TRANSPORTATION

The liaison collaborates with transportation directors to arrange services per district policy and federal law. When cross-district enrollment occurs, both districts must agree on cost-sharing or divide responsibility equally.

No mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools was found in this regulation.

## JFBA — Choice/Open Enrollment

POLICY OVERVIEW

The Poudre School District supports choice/open enrollment in grades K-12, allowing parents to apply for schools other than their assigned neighborhood school based on available space.

KEY DEFINITIONS

"Choice/open enrollment": Parent-initiated enrollment in a different K-12 school or program than currently assigned.

"Option school or program": A school without a neighborhood attendance area.

"Program": IB, Core Knowledge, Expeditionary Learning, or Bilingual programs.

GENERAL RULES

Enrollment eligibility requires residence verification or approved choice/open enrollment authorization. Once admitted to a choice school, students may remain "for all grades served at the choice school or program without" annual reapplication.

Overcrowding Protocol: Non-resident students face reassignment first, followed by resident students, based on "reverse chronological order of their admission."

Mid-Year Changes: Students must remain at their choice school for the school year. Mid-year transfers require new applications subject to space availability.

District Limitations: The district need not alter school structures, establish new programs, waive eligibility criteria, create additional space, or enroll non-residents after "pupil enrollment count day."

DENIAL CRITERIA

Applications may be denied for:
1. Lack of space or teaching staff
2. Requested school lacks appropriate programs
3. Student fails to meet established eligibility criteria
4. Student expelled within preceding 12 months or engaged in behavior "detrimental to the welfare or safety of other students"
5. Student has completed secondary education
6. Overcrowding projections in attendance area

APPLICATION PROCESS

Applications must be submitted online through the district website. Separate applications are required for each school and child, except multiple birth siblings submit one joint application per school.

Kindergarten Exception: Students must register at their neighborhood school even with pending choice applications, ensuring adequate staffing projections.

SPACE AVAILABILITY DETERMINATION

After the initial application period closes, available spaces are calculated based on "projected enrollment of neighborhood students" and program capacity.

Students Not Requiring Applications:
- Neighborhood school attendees
- Employees' children (except for option schools)
- Students remaining at current choice schools
- Military children (with specific provisions)

PRIORITY ORDER FOR SPACE ALLOCATION

When applications exceed available spaces, priority follows this sequence:
1. In-district residents returning mid-year or continuing programs across levels
2. In-district new applicants with siblings at requested school
3. Employee-sponsored applicants (for option schools or mid-year transfers)
4. Other in-district new applicants
5. Out-of-district continuing program students across levels
6. Out-of-district new applicants with siblings at school
7. Other out-of-district new applicants

"If the number of choice/open enrollment applicants exceeds...within any of the foregoing priority levels...the order of priority within that level will be determined by a lottery."

ELIGIBILITY DETERMINATION

Eligibility assessments occur for applicants with available space. Out-of-district students with disabilities require "a written contract with the District for the payment of tuition to cover excess costs."

ACCEPTANCE & RESCISSION

Applicants offered seats must accept or reject within specified timeframes. The district may rescind enrollment for misrepresentation, overcrowding, program discontinuation, inability to meet special needs, failure to maintain eligibility, or "other reasons authorized by law."

WAITLIST MANAGEMENT

First-round applicants denied space are waitlisted by priority level. Second-round applicants are ordered by priority level plus "date and time of when the application was submitted." Waitlists are updated continuously and "purged annually on the date stated on the District's website."

MILITARY CHILDREN

Inbound active-duty military members may use "school liaison office address" for applications without additional documentation. Enrolled military children receive "guaranteed automatic matriculation while the child remains in the District," including advancement to "next grade, even if the next grade is in a different school level."

TRANSPORTATION

Students provide their own transportation unless state or federal law compliance requires district provision, including for "students experiencing homelessness and students with disabilities."

SPECIAL PROTECTIONS

The district cannot inquire about IEP or disability status "until after the applicant has been admitted." Non-discrimination protections apply to race, color, creed, religion, national origin, ancestry, sex, sexual orientation, gender identity, and disability.

APPEALS

"Appeals regarding the application of this policy...must be made to the superintendent, and the superintendent's decision and order (if any) is final."

The lottery mechanism used to break ties within priority levels is a randomized allocation process; no automated scoring, algorithmic ranking, AI, or predictive tool is described — allocation is by lottery and rule-based priority order.

## JFC — Student Withdrawal From School/Dropouts

CORE PURPOSE

The district emphasizes that "a diploma assists students to lead healthy and productive lives" and calls on all stakeholders to keep students enrolled through graduation.

PREVENTION AND INTERVENTION RESPONSIBILITIES

Principals and counselors must prioritize dropout prevention through direct student engagement and targeted programming, aiming to help at-risk learners return to school with minimal disruption.

NOTIFICATION REQUIREMENTS

When the district learns a student has dropped out, it must notify parents/guardians in writing to reinforce the importance of completing high school.

REQUIRED PROCEDURES FOR AT-RISK STUDENTS

Step 1 - Initial Meeting: "The student and a school counselor shall meet for the purpose of discussing the reason(s) for leaving school and the student's plans."

Step 2 - Formal Review: All parties—student, parents/guardians, counselor, and principal—review information and recommendations, then create a formal plan based on chosen options.

Step 3 - Final Notice: If the student persists in withdrawing, the principal issues written notice to parents of students 17+ years old, including encouragement to return, consequences of dropout status, and available resources (GED programs, counseling, addiction treatment, family services).

DROPOUT DEFINITION

Per Colorado statute, students absent six consecutive weeks or more annually are classified as dropouts and reported to the Department of Education, unless they later enroll elsewhere, attend an alternative program, or are present by year-end.

No mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools was found in this policy.

## JGA — Assignment of New Students to Classes and Grade Levels

ENROLLMENT AND CREDIT

Students from accredited public or private schools are "placed in grades and classes on the basis of their grade placement/credits in the school from which they are transferring."

Home schooled students must "apply at the school in their attendance area at least two weeks before the beginning of the first or second semester" to allow time for assessment and placement by the principal.

STUDENTS IN GRADES K-8

The district may assess home schooled students "for the purpose of placing the student in the proper grade." Parents should provide "results from district-approved standardized tests when available." The principal determines "appropriate grade placement" based on test results and parent-provided information.

STUDENTS IN GRADES 9-12

Accreditation and Credit: Home schooled students from accredited programs "shall be evaluated for credit with the procedure used for transfer students." Principals may require "documentation of the student's course of study" and may recommend "additional study" if knowledge gaps exist.

Senior Year Requirement: Students must "successfully complete the final semester of their senior year in a district high school in order to graduate." This allows time for "assessments (including testing, observations, and other performance criteria)."

Grade Recording: Grades transfer as "S" (satisfactory) and "U" (unsatisfactory).

Athletics: Students are "subject to Colorado High School Activities Association (CHSAA) rules."

Notification: Principals shall meet with parents to explain placement decisions upon request.

APPEAL PROCEDURES

Written appeal to principal within one week. Principal responds within two weeks. Second appeal to Assistant Superintendent within one week. Final response within two weeks.

No mention of automated decision systems, artificial intelligence, machine learning, or predictive scoring tools was found in this policy. Placement relies on standardized test results, transfer credits, and human (principal) determination; "assessments (including testing, observations, and other performance criteria)" are administered and evaluated by school staff, not an automated system.

## JHCA — Open/Closed Campus

GENERAL CAMPUS ACCESS RULE

"All schools, except the comprehensive senior high schools, shall operate under a closed-campus policy." Students cannot leave during the school day without authorization.

SENIOR HIGH SCHOOL EXCEPTION

Open-campus represents a privilege available to senior high school students, with regulations determined by each principal.

VISITOR REQUIREMENTS

All visitors must "check in at the administrative office immediately upon arrival." Unauthorized individuals may be asked to leave and warned about potential trespassing citations. Non-compliant visitors may be referred to law enforcement.

ELEMENTARY AND MIDDLE SCHOOL PROVISIONS

Students may depart for lunch with written parental requests submitted to the principal. If another adult takes students to lunch, written permission from those students' guardians is required.

When parents/guardians pick up students, they must sign them out at the office. Students returning the same day must sign back in. "No student will be dismissed early without permission from their parent(s) or legal guardian(s)."

DOCUMENT HISTORY

This policy was adopted February 1974 and revised multiple times, with the most recent superintendent revision on May 4, 2009.

No mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools was found in this policy.

## JH_JHB — STUDENT ATTENDANCE/TRUANCY

State law mandates that parents ensure children receive adequate education and attend school if of compulsory age. The district emphasizes communication and collaboration between school and home to maintain safety and increase student success.

DEFINITIONS

Habitually Truant means a student aged six years or older (by August 1) and under seventeen years of age who has either four total days of unexcused absences from public school in any one calendar month, or ten total days of unexcused absences from public school during the reported school year.

Tardiness means a student entering classes after the scheduled start time without proper excuse.

Unexcused Absence means an absence without a valid, verifiable excuse from a parent consistent with this policy, or an unauthorized departure of a student from school or from a class.

EXCUSED ABSENCES

The policy enumerates circumstances considered excused, including: absences approved in advance by a school administrator (prearranged whenever possible); medical or dental appointments or serious matters that cannot be scheduled outside school hours; temporary illness or injury; extended absence due to disability or behavioral health concerns; participation in a work-study program; district-sponsored activities; religious or cultural observance; court-ordered custody exchanges or required legal appearances; suspension; expulsion (in which case alternative education is to be made available); and therapy, medical, or legal appointments related to harassment or discrimination. The district may require proof regarding any of the above exceptions.

UNEXCUSED ABSENCES

Each unexcused absence is entered on the student's record. The school must notify parents in a timely manner. The district may impose appropriate penalties for unexcused absences. Students identified under the McKinney-Vento Act receive special consideration in application of attendance consequences. Students or parents may petition the principal for exceptions, which require assistant superintendent approval.

TRUANCY MONITORING AND INTERVENTION

Parents are notified annually of the compulsory attendance obligations under state law and must provide current contact information to the district. The district establishes a monitoring system to track excused and unexcused absences. When an absence occurs without parent notification, school employees (or volunteers acting under the direction of school personnel) will make a reasonable effort to notify the parent or caregiver by telephone. The district will meet with parents to review the reasons for truancy. A plan must be developed for any student who is declared habitually truant, based on the definition above. Judicial proceedings may be initiated against habitually truant students and/or their parents as authorized under state compulsory-attendance law.

MAKE-UP WORK

Make-up work is provided for excused absences and for suspensions, unless the principal determines otherwise. Make-up work is not provided during expulsion; instead, alternative education services are to be offered. Following unexcused absences, make-up work is allowed but the student may receive only partial credit. Students are allowed one day to make up work for each day of absence, unless an accommodation extends the timeline. The student is responsible for picking up missed assignments on the day of return.

TARDINESS

Penalties will be imposed for excessive tardiness because of the disruption it causes. Parents are notified of all such penalties. Students who present passes from other staff members are not considered tardy, and teachers must honor those passes.

APPEALS

Appeals of attendance/truancy determinations are directed to the assistant superintendent's office. If unresolved at that level, a further appeal may be made to the superintendent, whose decision is final.

AUTOMATED SYSTEMS / AI NOTE

No automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools are mentioned anywhere in this policy document.

## JIBA — Student Government

POLICY OVERVIEW

This policy establishes student council operations as primarily advisory. The document encourages councils to voice opinions on matters including curriculum, rules, organization, facility use, and school activities.

AUTHORITY & DECISION-MAKING

The principal and student council sponsor are directed to create operational rules and guidelines. The principal may delegate decision-making to students regarding "student clubs and organizations, awards, social activities, community projects, and student body organization and election."

RATIFICATION REQUIREMENTS

All student activities and organizations require approval by either the principal or student council sponsor before proceeding.

REVISION HISTORY

Adopted: April 1972. Revised: June 1982, August 1982, June 1988, January 27, 1997.

No mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools was found in this policy. No specific election procedures are prescribed in the source document.

## JICA — Student Dress

OPENING STATEMENT

The district recognizes that students and parents bear primary responsibility for dress choices. The policy emphasizes equitable enforcement without disparities based on protected characteristics including "race, color, creed, national origin, ancestry, sex, sexual orientation, gender identity, gender expression, marital status, age or disability."

DEFINITIONS

Apparel: "clothing; headwear; facial coverings; accessories such as scarves, sunglasses, or jewelry; shoes."

Grooming: "makeup, tattoos, and hair style."

Dress code: "the set of parameters established within this Policy that describes the standards for student attire and grooming."

GENERAL PRINCIPLES

The policy aims to "reduce discipline problems and maintain school order and safety." Students should dress appropriately for school activities. Cleanliness is expected for health reasons. Apparel must not interfere with or endanger students during classroom participation.

Coverage requirement: "Apparel must be worn in a way that covers breasts, nipples, genitals, midriff, and buttocks."

MANDATORY CLOTHING

Students must wear: (1) a shirt covering front, back, and sides; (2) pants or equivalent; and (3) shoes.

PROHIBITED ITEMS

Students cannot wear apparel displaying "symbols of hate or speech that expresses animus or violence," sexually suggestive content, swimsuits (outside class), face-covering items, or "gang-related" attire.

ENFORCEMENT

Violations trigger notification and three options: provide own alternative clothing, wear school-provided clothing, or contact parents. Repeated violations may result in suspension or expulsion.

No mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools was found in this policy.

## JICDD — Threat Assessments

AUTHORITY AND SCOPE

The district may conduct threat assessments for potentially dangerous student behaviors occurring on district property, through district networks, in district vehicles, at district events, or off-campus when reasonably connected to school activities.

POLICY PURPOSE

The threat assessment serves three objectives:
1. "Gain an understanding of the elements of the threat/risk so that imminent and/or serious dangers can be identified"
2. "Disable risk factors for violence"
3. "Respond, manage, and support the student of concern and/or others involved"

KEY PROVISIONS

Safety is identified as a district priority for students, staff, and community. Students whose "behavior, actions and/or communication may pose a threat" should be evaluated per district procedures. Law enforcement assistance is sought when information warrants it.

SPECIAL POPULATIONS

Students with IDEA and Section 504 protections may undergo assessment "in accordance with governing law."

RECORDS AND INFORMATION SHARING

The policy acknowledges that schools generally need parental consent to release student records, but permits disclosure of threat assessments "without parental consent" when necessary to protect health or safety during emergencies.

No mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring, risk-rating tools, or predictive assessment tools was found in this policy. The threat assessment process as described relies on human evaluation by district personnel and, where warranted, law enforcement, rather than an automated or algorithmic system.

## JICDE — Bullying Prevention and Education

STATEMENT OF PURPOSE

The board aims to create a secure school climate free from bullying. The policy applies to all district property, sanctioned activities, district transportation, and off-campus conduct with reasonable school connection.

PROHIBITED BEHAVIORS

The policy prohibits: bullying itself, retaliation against reporters, and knowingly false accusations.

KEY DEFINITIONS

Bullying: Described as intentionally causing "physical, mental, or emotional harm" or using coercion to control others, occurring through written, verbal, electronic, or physical means. It includes conduct based on protected characteristics like disability, race, sex, sexual orientation, gender identity, or religion.

Three Types: Physical (bodily harm), verbal (harmful communication), and relational/social (harming relationships or status).

Three Components: Bullying requires intent, repetition or likelihood thereof, and a power imbalance.

Cyberbullying: Off-campus electronic bullying may fall within district authority when affecting educational access or school connection exists.

What Bullying Is Not: Teasing, conflict, or fights lacking power imbalances, unless the teasing is hostile and causes harm.

PREVENTION AND INTERVENTION GOALS

The district commits to: sending clear anti-bullying messages, annual staff training, immediate intervention procedures, behavioral change efforts, parent partnerships, supporting targets, developing peer support networks, promoting positive school climate, establishing advisory teams, surveying students, involving students in prevention, providing resources including social-emotional learning, and engaging restorative practices while avoiding mediation for bullying situations.

REPORTING REQUIREMENTS

Students and staff must report bullying to administrators, counselors, or teachers. All staff witnessing bullying must immediately intervene and report to the principal.

INVESTIGATION AND RESPONSE

Principals must promptly investigate reports. For students with IEPs or 504 Plans, investigations must determine whether the bullying affected their free appropriate public education, with team convening and plan revision as needed.

DISCIPLINE AND SUPPORTS

Consequences range up to suspension or expulsion, alongside educational interventions, parent conferences, and staff training on recognizing and addressing bullying.

No mention of automated monitoring systems, artificial intelligence, predictive scoring tools, or technology-based surveillance mechanisms for identifying bullying behavior was found in this policy. "Cyberbullying" is addressed only as a mode/medium of bullying (electronic communication), not as a technological detection or monitoring system.

## JICEC — Student Distribution of Non-School Materials

The policy grants students the right to distribute non-school materials on campus or at school events, subject to specific restrictions and procedures. "Non-school materials" refers to items not owned or sanctioned by the District that aren't used in curricular or extracurricular programs.

PROHIBITED CONTENT

Students cannot distribute materials that "create or threaten to create substantial disruption of District or school operations," disrupt the learning environment, cause injury to persons or property, are obscene, defamatory, or violate privacy rights, or encourage violation of laws or District policies.

APPROVAL PROCESS

Distributions exceeding 10 copies require principal notification at least three school days prior, with sample materials provided. The principal responds within three days with written approval or denial and explanation.

DISTRIBUTION PARAMETERS

Time: limited to 30 minutes before school, during lunch, or 15 minutes after school only. Place: designated locations only; never in classrooms or during activities. Manner: no coercion allowed; students must remove or recycle leftover materials.

APPEALS

Decisions may be appealed to the assistant superintendent within 10 days; the assistant superintendent's decision is final. This is the only human-review/appeal step identified in the policy.

DISCIPLINE

Violations of this policy shall be grounds for student discipline and denial of future distribution requests. No automated, algorithmic, AI, machine-learning, scoring, or predictive tools are mentioned anywhere in this policy.

## JICF — Secret Societies/Gang Activity Dress

The Board prohibits student involvement in, promotion of, or membership indication in secret societies or gangs "on all District property, on all District vehicles, at all District or school-sponsored activities or events, and off District property when such conduct has a reasonable connection to the District."

KEY DEFINITIONS

Gang: "a group of three or more individuals with a common interest, bond or activity characterized by criminal or delinquent conduct." Delinquent Conduct: "conduct of a juvenile contrary to any federal or state law, any county or municipal ordinance except traffic offenses, or any lawful order of a court." Secret Society: "a group that possesses secret rites among its members and/or deals with private matters known only to one or a few and kept from others."

NON-DISCRIMINATION PROTECTION

District employees cannot infer gang or secret society membership "solely because of ethnic background, race, color, religion, socioeconomic status, or any other protected class."

ADMINISTRATIVE ACTIONS

School administrators must contact parents regarding possible involvement and may implement disciplinary measures as appropriate. The superintendent maintains communication with law enforcement and community agencies for information sharing.

No provisions regarding formal discipline procedures, hearings, appeals, automated decision systems, or algorithmic decision-making appear in this policy document.

## JICH — Student Conduct Involving Drugs and Alcohol

The Poudre School District maintains a policy addressing student conduct related to drugs and alcohol through education, support, and enforcement mechanisms.

KEY DEFINITIONS

"Analogue": a substance producing stimulant, depressant, or hallucinogenic effects similar to controlled substances. "Drugs": substances defined as drugs or controlled substances under state/federal law, including analogues. "Authorized drugs": over-the-counter and prescription medications "properly possessed and used by the person for whom they are intended." "Unauthorized drugs": all drugs not classified as authorized. "Distributing": "a student's involvement in the exchange of drugs for anything of value."

PROHIBITED CONDUCT

The policy prohibits "possession of drug paraphernalia and possession, use, distribution, or being under the influence of alcohol, unauthorized drugs" on district property, vehicles, and at sponsored events.

DISCIPLINARY FRAMEWORK

First offense: five-day suspension with up to four days deferred upon completion of counseling or drug/alcohol programs. Second offense: ten-day suspension with up to five days deferred. Third offense: student considered for expulsion; may participate in intervention programs to avoid expulsion. Fourth and subsequent offenses: recommended for expulsion. Distribution: "a student will be considered for expulsion for distribution of any drugs on the first and any subsequent offenses."

The policy includes no mentions of automated systems, algorithms, AI, machine learning, scoring, or predictive tools.

## JICI — Student Conduct Involving Weapons

The school board prohibits student possession, use, or threatened use of dangerous weapons on district property, vehicles, and school-sponsored events, as well as off-campus conduct with reasonable school connection.

KEY DEFINITIONS

Dangerous weapon includes: firearms (loaded or unloaded), projectile devices such as pellet guns, "any knife, regardless of blade length," and objects intended to inflict death or serious bodily injury. Firearm encompasses weapons designed to expel projectiles via explosives, frames/receivers, mufflers, silencers, and destructive devices.

MANDATORY CONSEQUENCES

Federal law requires "expulsion for no less than one full calendar year" for students bringing firearms to school. The superintendent may modify this requirement case-by-case in writing — this is a human-review/discretion step for the mandatory expulsion determination.

FIREARM FACSIMILES

Students cannot carry items resembling actual firearms without prior principal authorization. Violations may result in suspension or expulsion. The principal's decision on facsimile authorization is "final and not subject to appeal" — i.e., no appeal process exists for this determination.

VOLUNTARY SURRENDER OPTION

Students may surrender prohibited weapons (excluding firearms) to administrators without discipline if brought inadvertently without intent to use. Firearms always result in disciplinary consequences regardless of circumstances. Repeated surrenders may trigger discipline.

LAW ENFORCEMENT REFERRAL

District personnel shall refer any student to law enforcement who possesses firearms or dangerous weapons on campus or at school events. No automated, algorithmic, AI, or predictive determination process is mentioned; weapon determinations are made by administrators/principals.

## JICJ — Student Possession and Use of Personal Communication Devices

The Board of Education acknowledges that personal technology devices (PTDs) can serve educational purposes and facilitate emergency communication, but restricts their use during the school day to "reduce distractions, support learning, and promote safety." Accommodations for disabilities are exempt from these limitations.

KEY DEFINITIONS

An "emergency situation" involves threats to life, injury, or property damage. "Personal technology device" encompasses cell phones, tablets, laptops, GPS devices, cameras, wearables, and similar portable electronics.

USAGE RESTRICTIONS

Students may use PTDs on school property only when use causes no "distraction or disruption to the learning environment"; during instruction, use must connect to curricula and receive explicit staff authorization; in classrooms/educational activities, use must be expressly authorized by administrators; on district vehicles, use must not interfere with safe operation. Emergency exceptions apply when PTD use doesn't impede emergency response. Recording in bathrooms or locker rooms is prohibited.

PERMITTED ACCOMMODATIONS

PTD use is allowed as a disability accommodation or English-language support when documented in a student's plan per Colorado and federal law.

CONSEQUENCES FOR VIOLATIONS

Violations may result in device confiscation (duration determined by principal, potentially through the school year), academic penalties (failing grades for cheating/dishonesty), discipline including suspension or expulsion, and law enforcement referral for legal violations.

Adoption dates: April 13, 2010 (effective July 1, 2010); Revised June 9, 2026 (effective July 1, 2026). No automated, algorithmic, AI, machine-learning, scoring, or predictive monitoring tools are referenced; device-use decisions rest with staff/administrators.

## JIH — Searches

The policy establishes procedures for student searches to protect "the health, safety and welfare of students, staff, and members of the community."

KEY DEFINITIONS

Unauthorized: items dangerous to health/safety, disruptive to school functions, or described as unauthorized in school rules. Reasonable Suspicion: requires "specific and articulable facts and reasonable inferences" that a search will reveal policy violations; the standard demands both justification at inception and searches "reasonably related in scope to the circumstances."

SEARCH AUTHORITY CATEGORIES

Lockers/Desks: school property subject to "general inspections" without notice, consent, or warrant. Vehicles: interiors and trunks may be searched upon reasonable suspicion of illegal items. Personal Searches: permitted when reasonable suspicion exists; safety checks conducted as part of documented safety plans require a "private" setting with "at least one other District employee" present. Canine Inspections: trained dogs may sniff lockers, vehicles, and students; an alert "can constitute reasonable suspicion for a search." Metal Detectors: authorized when reasonable suspicion exists, following a pattern of weapons, or after violence incidents.

PROCEDURAL PROTECTIONS

More intrusive searches beyond removing outer clothing require "law enforcement officer" involvement. Weapon possession triggers mandatory law enforcement contact.

EVIDENCE HANDLING

Seized items are tagged, secured, and held until "no longer needed" for disciplinary matters, after which parents may retrieve items or administrators dispose of them. All search-decision authority in this policy rests with human staff/administrators/law enforcement; no automated, algorithmic, AI, or predictive determination system is mentioned.

## JJ — Interscholastic Athletic Training and Personal Conduct Rules

This policy establishes conduct standards for student athletes in grades 9-12 participating in district interscholastic sports programs. The document emphasizes that athletic participation is "a privilege, not a right" and requires year-round compliance.

GENERAL REQUIREMENTS

Student athletes must comply with district Code of Conduct policies, Colorado High School Activities Association bylaws, and individual team rules. They remain ineligible during any suspension or expulsion period.

SUBSTANCE AND TOBACCO PROHIBITIONS

The policy prohibits "use or possess[ion of] any beverage containing alcohol," tobacco products, or controlled substances on a year-round basis, including off-campus and non-school activities.

VIOLATION CONSEQUENCES

First violation: 30% competition suspension. Second violation: 50% competition suspension. Third violation: permanent athletic ineligibility through graduation. During suspensions, athletes must maintain practice participation and "remain in good standing" with their teams.

This policy contains no mention of automated decision systems, algorithms, AI tools, machine learning, predictive scoring, technology monitoring, formal hearing procedures, or appeals processes for violations.

## JJG — Contests for Students

This policy establishes procedures for reviewing student contest participation requests. A panel including the assistant superintendent of school services evaluates proposals.

KEY APPROVAL CRITERIA

Participation in the contest by students or classes must be relevant to the major objectives of the district; appropriate grade level alignment with instructional programs; deadlines that don't disrupt normal instruction; noncommercial nature of the activity; student motivation enhancement; positive public relations value; self-image building for participants; compliance with Colorado High School Activities Association guidelines for senior high events.

RECOMMENDED OUTCOMES

Three options: full-scale participation across appropriate classes, voluntary teacher/student participation, or no participation.

Adopted February 1975; revised December 1982, May 1988, and October 28, 1996. This document contains no references to discipline procedures, hearings, appeals, student data privacy, technology monitoring, automated systems, algorithms, artificial intelligence, or predictive tools.

## JJID — Student Physicals for School Athletics

Students participating in middle or high school competitive athletics must obtain an "annual physical examination prior to any competition or formal practice" related to their participation.

EXAMINATION REQUIREMENTS

Must be performed by a practitioner licensed in the United States; must be recognized by CHSAA as qualified to perform sports physicals; must include "the issuance of a written and signed certification as required by CHSAA."

ADOPTION AND REVISION HISTORY

Adopted by Board: January 11, 1999. Revised by Board: September 10, 2001. Revised by Superintendent: June 16, 2009.

This policy contains no provisions related to discipline, hearings, appeals, human review, student data monitoring, technology systems, automated decision-making, algorithms, artificial intelligence, machine learning, scoring systems, or predictive tools.

## JK — Student Discipline

Policy JK addresses student discipline and rights of students with disabilities, along with procedures for disciplining students with disabilities.

OBJECTIVE AND APPROACH

The policy establishes that discipline aims to maintain safe schools and help students develop self-control. The district emphasizes "restorative practices" and positive learning environments, considering prevention and intervention approaches where feasible.

DEFINITIONS

Behavioral intervention plan (BIP): a plan for students with IEPs addressing inappropriate behaviors. Restorative practices: methods emphasizing repair of harm caused by misconduct. Serious bodily injury: involves substantial risk of death, extreme pain, disfigurement, or functional loss.

EXPULSION PREVENTION

Expulsion should be a last resort. Districts must consider alternative remedies and provide support services to at-risk students through partnerships with community organizations.

DISCIPLINARY INFORMATION SHARING

Principals must communicate discipline details to teachers and counselors having direct contact with the student, while maintaining confidentiality under FERPA guidelines — this is the policy's central provision governing use of student (discipline) data.

STUDENTS WITH DISABILITIES — KEY PROVISIONS

Students with disabilities may be suspended up to 10 school days annually. Beyond 10 days constitutes a "disciplinary change of placement" requiring educational services. A "manifestation determination" must occur within 10 school days to assess whether behavior relates to the disability — this is a required human-review step performed by a team (not an automated process).

SPECIAL CIRCUMSTANCES FOR ALTERNATIVE PLACEMENT

The district may place students with disabilities in alternative settings for up to 45 days without manifestation review if the student carried weapons, possessed illegal drugs, sold controlled substances, or inflicted serious bodily injury.

SUSPECTED DISABILITIES

Students suspected of having disabilities receive protections if the district has "knowledge" through written parent concerns or teacher observations. No automated, algorithmic, AI, machine-learning, scoring, or predictive tools are referenced anywhere in this policy; all determinations (manifestation determination, placement, discipline) are made by human staff/teams.

## JKA — Physical Intervention, Restraint, Seclusion and Time-Out

This district policy establishes procedures for physical intervention, restraint, seclusion, and time-out with students to maintain safe learning environments. Actions complying with this policy are not considered child abuse or corporal punishment.

PHYSICAL INTERVENTION

District employees may physically intervene without constituting "restraint" when "reasonably necessary to protect persons or property, prevent an act of wrongdoing and/or maintain student discipline."

KEY DEFINITIONS

Restraint: methods limiting freedom of movement, excluding physician-recommended devices, protective equipment, adaptive devices, and medical positioning devices. Seclusion: isolated placement preventing egress. Time-out: non-exclusionary removal of reinforcers, or exclusionary separation at three levels (contingent observation, exclusion, isolation).

RESTRAINT GUIDELINES

Employees may use physical restraint in non-face-down positions when students pose a "serious, probable and imminent threat of bodily injury." Face-down restraints and mechanical/chemical restraints are prohibited except for students displaying deadly weapons. Restraint must not impede breathing or communication; must be administered only by trained employees; must be released within fifteen minutes except for safety reasons; and requires continuous monitoring.

SECLUSION GUIDELINES

Permissible only when students pose a "serious, probable and imminent threat of bodily injury." Rooms must be adequately lit, minimum 6x6 feet, with constant observation by qualified staff immediately outside.

TRAINING, NOTIFICATION, AND REVIEW

Staff using restraint require training including de-escalation and physical management techniques, with retraining every two years. Parents/guardians must be notified in writing before potential use. Written incident reports must be submitted within one school day. Comprehensive reviews are documented annually by the superintendent. All decisions to restrain/seclude are made by trained human staff in the moment; no automated, algorithmic, AI, or predictive tool is mentioned.

## JKB — Detention of Students

This district policy permits school administrators to detain students after regular hours as a disciplinary alternative to suspension or exclusion.

GROUNDS FOR DETENTION

Detention may be imposed for "violation of the code of conduct and behavior that is disruptive," per District Policy JK regarding student discipline.

NOTIFICATION REQUIREMENT

"The parent/caregiver must be notified of the detention one school day prior to the student's detention."

SUPERVISION REQUIREMENTS

"Students who serve a detention after school must never be left alone during their detention. Their supervision must be provided or arranged for by an administrator or designee."

ADMINISTRATIVE AUTHORITY

"A school administrator or designee may detain a student for disciplinary reasons after school hours as an alternative to exclusionary discipline such as a suspension."

Adopted: November 25, 1996. Revised: June 9, 2026 and July 1, 2026. Cross-referenced with JK — Student Discipline and Rights of Students with Disabilities/Procedures Regarding Discipline of Students with Disabilities. This policy contains no references to automated decision systems, algorithms, AI, machine learning, or predictive tools; the detention decision is made solely by a human administrator or designee.

## JKBA — Disciplinary Removal from Classroom

This district policy governs how teachers may remove students from classrooms for disruptive conduct. Removals constitute in-school suspensions and typically last one to three days.

KEY DEFINITIONS

Disciplinary removal: "removal of a student from the teacher's classroom to another location at the school after the day on which the disruptive behavior occurs." Willful behavior: conscious, purposeful actions where students know or disregard whether disruption will result. Material and substantial disruption: actions causing "serious interference with the orderly operation of the school."

REQUIRED PROCEDURES

Before imposing removal, teachers must consult administrators, who make the final determination on appropriateness — establishing a mandatory human-review step by an administrator before removal is finalized. Students and families must receive notice of reasons and duration.

BEHAVIOR PLAN REQUIREMENTS

First removal: develop a behavior plan with student and family input. Second removal: review and revise the plan; assess whether the school failed to implement it. Third and subsequent removals (secondary level): removal may extend through the grading term, only after written disclosure in the prior behavior plan.

STUDENT PROTECTIONS

Students receive coursework during removal periods. Those removed through the grading term may "complete and receive equivalent credit for the course." Special education students are protected under separate policies addressing IEP and Section 504 plan requirements.

Originally adopted August 14, 2000; most recently revised June 9, 2026 (effective July 1, 2026). No automated, algorithmic, AI, machine-learning, scoring, or predictive tools are referenced; classroom-removal decisions are made by teachers and administrators.

## JKC — Discipline of Habitually Disruptive Students

The policy defines a "habitually disruptive student" as one who has caused "a material and substantial disruption on school grounds, at a District-sanctioned activity or event or in a District vehicle three or more times" within a school year. "Material and substantial disruption" refers to "behavior that causes a serious interference with the orderly operation of the school" or its educational capacity, including actions harmful to others' welfare or safety.

NOTIFICATION REQUIREMENTS

Students and parents receive written notice of each disruption counted toward the habitually disruptive designation, plus written and telephone notification of the definition and potential consequences.

BEHAVIOR PLANNING

Following the first or second counted disruption, school administrators may develop a behavior plan with teacher and staff input, encouraging parental participation. Plans should address the disruptive conduct and include goals, objectives, and timelines for behavioral modification.

PLAN DOCUMENTATION

The behavior plan informs the student of consequences including "discipline, including suspension or expulsion." Copies go to the student, parent, responsible staff member, and the student's file.

ONGOING MANAGEMENT

Additional disruptions are handled per the developed behavior plan if one exists.

CONSEQUENCES

Once declared habitually disruptive, the principal may "authorize a suspension or initiate expulsion proceedings" after reviewing the student's records and prior incidents — a human, records-based review performed by the principal. This policy contains no references to automated decision systems, algorithms, AI tools, or predictive scoring mechanisms; the habitually-disruptive determination and consequence decisions are made by school administrators based on documented incident counts, not by any automated system.

## JKD-JKE — Suspension/Expulsion of Students

This policy establishes procedures for suspending or expelling students while ensuring parental involvement and due process protections. The board considers six factors when making disciplinary decisions: the student's age; the student's disciplinary history; the student's eligibility as a student with a disability; and three others including violation severity, safety threats, and whether lesser interventions would suffice.

DISCIPLINARY ALTERNATIVES

Prior to suspension or expulsion, principals may employ alternatives including detention, in-school suspension, counseling, mental health support, restorative justice programs, behavioral plans, and "Participation in the District's restorative justice/restorative practices program."

SUSPENSION AUTHORITY

Principals can suspend third-grade and higher students for up to five school days under certain grounds, or ten days under others. For preschool through second grade, suspensions are limited to three days unless "a longer period of suspension is necessary to resolve the safety threat."

EXPULSION PROCEDURES

The superintendent holds authority to expel students for periods not exceeding one year. The policy requires that "the superintendent, in accordance with the limitations imposed by C.R.S. § 22-33-101 through -110" make expulsion determinations — expulsion is a human determination made by the superintendent. Students or parents may appeal expulsion decisions to the board in writing, providing a human appeal/review pathway.

POST-EXPULSION SERVICES

Within five days of expulsion, districts must notify families of educational services available to expelled students designed to "provide a second chance for the student to succeed."

This policy contains no mentions of AI, algorithms, predictive tools, automated decision systems, or technology monitoring in disciplinary processes.

## JKD-JKE-R — Procedures Regarding Suspension/Expulsion of Students

This school district policy establishes procedures for student discipline through suspension and expulsion.

SUSPENSION PROCEDURES

The policy requires "notice of the contemplated action" including "a statement of what the student is accused of doing" and "the basis for the allegation" before removal occurs, except in emergencies involving "danger to people or property or ongoing threat of disrupting the academic process." Students receive a due process meeting where they "explain their alleged conduct and surrounding circumstances" with opportunity to call witnesses. Following suspension decisions, parents receive written notification and the student may make up schoolwork "during the period of suspension for full credit."

EXPULSION PROCEDURES

The district must provide written notice and allow hearing requests within specified timeframes. The policy states the district bears "the burden to prove, based on a preponderance of the evidence, that the student's actions constitute grounds for expulsion." Expulsion hearings include several protections: a trained hearing officer without conflicts of interest, student representation by counsel or advocate, witness testimony opportunities, and audio recording of proceedings. "Formal rules of evidence will not apply" but testimony occurs "under oath." These provisions establish a full human hearing/appeal process (hearing officer, testimony, evidentiary burden) governing suspension and expulsion decisions.

SPECIAL RESTRICTIONS

Expelled students face enrollment prohibitions in schools where crime victims or their family members work or study, with specific limitations outlined in the policy.

The document contains no references to automated decision systems, algorithms, AI, machine learning, or predictive tools.

## JKDA-JKEA — Grounds for Suspension/Expulsion of Students

FOR THIRD GRADE AND HIGHER

Students face discipline for conduct including: property destruction, assault, weapons/drugs/alcohol violations, defiance of authority, behavior threatening safety, disruption of operations, threats of harm, repeated interference with education, false accusations, and being declared habitually disruptive. A "habitually disruptive student" is defined as one who has caused "a material and substantial disruption three or more times" during a school year on school grounds or at school events.

FOR PRESCHOOL THROUGH SECOND GRADE

Suspension/expulsion is limited to: weapons policy violations, drugs/alcohol policy violations, and "conduct that endangers the health or safety of others." The policy notes such action requires a determination that removal "would create a safety threat that cannot otherwise be addressed" — a human safety-threat determination required before removal of the youngest students.

ADDITIONAL GROUNDS

Under Colorado law, expulsion may be based on physical/mental disability preventing educational benefit or causing risk to other students' welfare.

This policy does not mention automated systems, algorithms, predictive tools, AI, machine learning, data monitoring, or technological decision-making in discipline processes. It contains no references to appeals procedures, hearings procedures, or human review mechanisms within the extracted text (procedural/hearing provisions are set out separately in Policy JKD-JKE-R).

## JLCB — IMMUNIZATION OF STUDENTS

The district's health services coordinator must provide parents/guardians with Colorado's standardized immunization document annually, including required and recommended vaccines, age guidelines, and the school's vaccination rates compared to state standards. Parents/guardians must also receive information about their right to seek an exemption from immunization requirements.

ATTENDANCE WITHOUT STANDARD IMMUNIZATION

Students may attend district schools without standard immunizations only if they present one of four alternatives:

1. Parental Authorization: a signed request for local health officials to administer immunizations.

2. Medical Exemption: a completed certificate of medical exemption from a licensed physician, qualified physician assistant, or advanced practice nurse documenting that immunization poses health risks, submitted once and kept on file.

3. Online Education Module: a certificate showing completion of the Colorado Department of Public Health and Environment module, signed by parent/guardian or eligible student, submitted annually.

4. Nonmedical Exemption: a certificate signed annually by both parent/guardian (or eligible student) and a healthcare provider, maintained on file each year.

COMMUNICABLE DISEASE PROVISION

During outbreaks of vaccine-preventable diseases, exempted students may be excluded from attendance.

ADOPTION/REVISION DATES

Adopted May 22, 1995; revised April 14, 1997, October 3, 2016, September 18, 2017, and July 22, 2021.

## JLCD — ADMINISTERING MEDICINE TO STUDENTS/ASTHMA, ALLERGY AND ANAPHYLAXIS

This policy governs medicine administration to students, excluding medical marijuana (covered under Policy JLCDB). Violations of possession, use, or distribution contrary to this policy constitute violations of Policy JICH regarding drugs and alcohol.

KEY DEFINITIONS

Emergency-use epinephrine is a portable, disposable drug delivery device approved by the Federal Food and Drug Administration that contains a premeasured, single dose. Medicine includes both prescription and nonprescription items. Nonprescription medicine encompasses over-the-counter products, homeopathic and herbal medicines, vitamins, and supplements. Opioid antagonist means naloxone or a similar FDA-approved non-controlled substance used to treat overdose. An Opioid-related Drug Overdose Event is an acute condition involving decreased consciousness or respiratory depression requiring medical assistance.

GENERAL ADMINISTRATION REQUIREMENTS

Students should take medicine outside school when possible. When necessary at school or school-sponsored activities, medicine may be administered by parents/caregivers or authorized District employees.

Written requests and liability releases signed by parent/caregiver and health care provider (MD, NP, PA, or DO) are mandatory. Separate documentation is required for each medicine and dosage change.

Verbal Request Exception: the school nurse may honor verbal requests only when prior written submission was impossible, the nurse confirms the caller's identity, and confirmation occurs with the prescribing provider. Written documentation must follow before second-day administration.

PRESCRIPTION MEDICINE RULES

Prescription medicine must arrive in original pharmacy-labeled containers displaying student name, medicine name, dosage, prescriber name, fill date, and expiration date.

Administration Authority: school nurses, nurse's designees (with principal approval and proper training), authorized high school students, and students authorized for asthma/allergy/anaphylaxis self-administration may administer prescription medicine. Administration requires written authorization and directions from the prescribing health care provider.

NONPRESCRIPTION MEDICINE RULES

Nonprescription medicine must arrive in original commercially-labeled containers.

Administration Authority: school nurses, nurse's designees (principal-approved), authorized high school students, and authorized asthma/allergy/anaphylaxis students may administer nonprescription medicine. Written authorization and directions from a treating health care provider are required for employee administration.

STUDENT SELF-ADMINISTRATION AUTHORIZATION

High school students and certain middle school students on international trips may possess and self-administer medicine (excluding medical marijuana and controlled substances) when: (1) a written parent/caregiver request and liability release are submitted; (2) required health care provider authorization is provided; (3) the school nurse and principal determine the student demonstrates proper administration ability, maturity, and responsibility; (4) the school nurse and principal determine possession poses no significant harm risk; (5) the student maintains only a one-day dose (unless the school nurse authorizes more for extended activities; insulin pumps are exempt from the one-day limit); (6) the student maintains medicine security; and (7) self-administration remains a revocable privilege.

ASTHMA, FOOD ALLERGY, AND ANAPHYLAXIS MANAGEMENT

Self-Carry Authorization Requirements: students with asthma, food allergies, or related life-threatening conditions may self-carry prescribed medication at school, school-sponsored activities, and in school vehicles when the following prerequisites are met: written medical authorization from the prescribing health care practitioner with medication name, purpose, dosage, frequency, and administration intervals; practitioner confirmation that the student received instruction and demonstrates capability; student demonstration of skill including identifying correct medication, demonstrating correct technique, understanding dosage and frequency, and recognizing administration timing; a written treatment plan developed by the school nurse collaborating with the health care practitioner (effective for the current school year or until a new plan is created); and a written contract signed by the school nurse, student, and parent/caregiver assigning responsibility levels.

Student Contract Requirements: the student will demonstrate medication competency and asthma/allergy management skills, notify officials when emergency medication is used or condition worsens, and not allow others to use the medication.

Parent/Caregiver Contract Requirements: the parent/caregiver will provide written medication orders and written authorization for possession and self-administration, ensure proper pharmacy/practitioner labeling, verify the device contains medication and hasn't expired, provide backup medication for emergencies, and regularly review allergy status with the student.

School Nurse Contract Requirements: the nurse will review correct technique with the student, be informed of dosage/timing orders and appropriate medication use, ensure regular status reviews, notify relevant employees on a need-to-know basis, and assign employee(s) to make 911 emergency calls if exposure requires epinephrine.

Liability Release: the parent/caregiver must sign a statement releasing the District, school, associated entities, employees, and volunteers from liability except for willful and wanton conduct or treatment plan disregard.

Emergency Epinephrine Administration Protocol: after emergency-use epinephrine administration at school, school-sponsored activities, or school vehicles, the student must immediately proceed to the school nurse. The school must promptly call 911, and authorized employees must provide appropriate follow-up care.

Food Allergy and Anaphylaxis Management: districts must make available on websites and at schools the Colorado Department of Public Health and Environment standard form allowing parents/caregivers to provide food allergy information. Schools must have plans for communication with emergency medical services, including sharing student food allergy forms with emergency responders. Parents/caregivers must supply prescribed medication for students not authorized to self-carry; emergency medications must be stored in secure, accessible locations for designated employees.

Employee Training: school nurses and principals must identify employees (particularly those working with allergic students) for emergency anaphylaxis treatment training covering food allergy understanding and reasonable avoidance measures, anaphylaxis symptom recognition, appropriate response procedures, and self-injectable epinephrine administration.

Health Plan Development: school nurses must develop Health Plans for each student with potential life-threatening food allergies, coordinating with IEP and Section 504 Plan teams if applicable. Plans must consider information from the student, parent/caregiver, and health care provider (including Food Allergy Forms) and include reasonable exposure-reduction steps.

Administrative Guidelines: health services coordinators must develop, review, and revise administrative guidelines for proper food allergy and anaphylaxis management. District employees working with allergic students must comply with these guidelines.

STOCK EMERGENCY-USE EPINEPHRINE

If supplies and funding permit, the District maintains a stock epinephrine supply for emergency anaphylaxis events on school grounds (excluding buses). Administration must comply with state law and State Board of Education rules. This stock supply does not replace student-specific medications provided by parents/caregivers.

OPIOID ANTAGONIST PROVISIONS

When funding and supplies are available, the District may maintain opioid antagonist stock supplies on school grounds, school buses, or both. District employees may administer opioid antagonists to students or others per state law, provided they receive appropriate training including overdose risk factors, overdose recognition, emergency services procedures, rescue breathing, and antagonist administration, developed by the health services coordinator. District employees may furnish opioid antagonists to any individual on school grounds or school buses. Employees administering opioid antagonists per policy and law are not subject to civil liability or criminal prosecution. School principal's designees may provide student training before furnishing opioid antagonists, and student possession or administration is not a policy violation.

SYNTHETIC OPIATE DETECTION TESTS

If funding and supplies are available, the District may maintain supplies of non-laboratory synthetic opiate detection tests and non-laboratory additive detection tests. District employees may furnish these tests to any individual per Student Services Department guidelines. Students may lawfully possess non-laboratory synthetic opiate detection tests or non-laboratory additive detection tests on school grounds, school buses, or at school-sponsored events without policy violation.

BOARD ADOPTION AND REVISION HISTORY

Adopted February 1975; revised February 1982, January 1986, May 1988, May 22, 1995, June 23, 1997; reviewed October 11, 2004; revised April 24, 2006, April 13, 2010 (effective July 1, 2010), June 14, 2016 (effective July 1, 2016), May 28, 2019 (effective July 1, 2019), May 24, 2022 (effective July 1, 2022), June 9, 2026 (effective July 1, 2026).

CROSS REFERENCES

JLCE, First Aid and Emergency Medical Care; JLCD-R, Administering Medicine to Students; JICH, Student Conduct Involving Drugs and Alcohol; JLCDB, Administering Medical Marijuana to Qualified Students on District Property.

LEGAL REFERENCES

C.R.S. 12-255-101 et seq. (Nurse and Nurse Aide Practice Act); C.R.S. 18-1-712 (immunity for administering opioid antagonist); C.R.S. 22-1-119 (liability for adverse drug reactions/side effects); C.R.S. 22-1-119.1 (stock opiate antagonist supplies); C.R.S. 22-1-119.3 (student prescription medication possession and administration; medical marijuana administration); C.R.S. 22-1-119.5 (Colorado School Children's Asthma, Food Allergy, and Anaphylaxis Management Act); C.R.S. 22-2-135 (Colorado School Children's Food Allergy and Anaphylaxis Management Act); C.R.S. 22-32-139 (food allergy and anaphylaxis policy requirement); 1 CCR 301-68 (student asthma/allergy/anaphylaxis and prescription medication possession and administration); 6 CCR 1010-6, Rule 9-105 (medication storage).

## JLCD-R — ADMINISTERING MEDICINE TO STUDENTS

These regulations exclude medical marijuana, which is governed separately under Policy JLCDB.

STORAGE OF MEDICINE

Medicines must be stored in the office or similar area at each school in a clean, locked cabinet, drawer, or other appropriate container restricted to medicine only and inaccessible to students. For refrigerated medicines, storage must be either (a) in a locked refrigerator reserved and used exclusively for the storage of medicine, or (b) in an impervious secondary container in a food storage refrigerator, separated from food and inaccessible to students.

At year's end, all stored medicine must be returned to the parent or guardian who furnished it or shall be appropriately disposed of by the school nurse.

HEALTH CARE PROVIDER AUTHORIZATION AND DIRECTIONS

Provider authorization must be written and signed, specifying: (a) the student's name; (b) name of the medicine; (c) purpose of the medicine; (d) dosage; (e) time(s) the medicine is to be administered; (f) manner of administration; (g) anticipated number of days the medicine needs to be administered at school and/or school-sponsored activities; and (h) possible side-effects.

SAFETY PRECAUTIONS

Before administration, personnel must verify: (a) a current request to administer medicine and release signed by the student's parent/guardian is on file; (b) written authorization and directions signed by the student's health care provider is on file; (c) the correct student is receiving the medicine; (d) the correct medicine is being administered; (e) the correct dose of medicine is being administered; (f) the medicine is being administered at the correct time; (g) the medicine has not expired; and (h) the medicine is being administered in the correct manner.

MEDICATION ERRORS

Errors include giving medicine to the wrong student, giving the wrong medicine to a student, giving the wrong dose, and related failures. Errors in administering medicine to a student must be promptly reported to the school nurse and to the student's parent or guardian, and must be documented on a Medication Error Report form. Critical errors require immediate contact with poison control or 911.

RECORDKEEPING

Individual records must include: (a) the name of the medicine; (b) the date and time the medicine was administered; (c) the dosage administered; (d) the manner the medicine was administered; (e) any unusual reactions or responses of the student; and (f) the name of the person administering the medicine. Records are maintained until the year of the student's twenty-first birthday.

ADOPTION AND REVISION DATES

Adopted: May 1994; revised May 22, 1995; January 26, 1998; April 24, 2006; May 23, 2016 (effective July 1, 2016); May 26, 2022 (effective July 1, 2022); reviewed October 11, 2004.

CROSS REFERENCE

JLCDB, Administering Medical Marijuana to Qualified Students on District Property.

## JLCDB — ADMINISTERING MEDICAL MARIJUANA TO QUALIFIED STUDENTS

This district policy establishes procedures for administering medical marijuana to qualified students, complying with Colorado law while protecting federal funding eligibility.

KEY DEFINITIONS

Designated location means a location identified in writing by the District in its sole discretion, including school grounds, buses, or sponsored events. Medical marijuana means cannabis products with THC concentration exceeding 0.3 percent used for addressing debilitating medical conditions. Permissible forms are non-smokeable products such as oils, tinctures, edible products or lotions administered and absorbed quickly; patches may be approved case-by-case. Primary caregiver means a parent, guardian, or licensed healthcare provider employed by the family, subject to visitor policies. Qualified student means one holding a valid physician recommendation and state registration, unable to receive treatment outside school hours. Volunteer means a District employee voluntarily administering marijuana per a written plan.

ADMINISTRATION REQUIREMENTS

Medical marijuana administration requires: (1) parent-provided physician recommendation and Colorado state registration documentation maintained in confidential medical records; (2) parent assumption of responsibility and liability release; (3) primary caregiver provision of permissible forms; (4) a District determination that location and method present no educational disruption or student exposure risk; (5) District storage options in locked containers with emergency accessibility, or immediate caregiver removal; (6) parental retrieval coordination, with District disposal if unclaimed reasonably; and (7) a written plan developed jointly identifying form, location, physician instructions, and protocols, signed by administrator, student (if capable), and parent.

RESTRICTIONS AND LIMITATIONS

This policy grants no right to demand access to specific locations. Federal property restrictions apply. Student possession or self-administration is strictly prohibited. The policy suspends immediately if federal funding loss is documented. Non-compliance results in revocation of administration privileges.

VOLUNTARY EMPLOYEE PARTICIPATION

Employees cannot be required, pressured, or directed to volunteer. School nurses retain discretion regarding involvement. Absence of a designated volunteer does not obligate District provision of alternatives.

ADOPTION/REVISION DATES

Adopted: May 24, 2022; Revised: June 9, 2026.

## JLCDC — MEDICALLY NECESSARY TREATMENT IN SCHOOL SETTING

This policy governs how districts must handle medically necessary treatment for students provided by private health-care specialists, ensuring compliance with federal disability law while maintaining district authority over school operations.

DEFINITIONS

Medically necessary treatment is care that a Colorado licensed health-care provider acting within the scope of the health-care provider's license recommends or orders. The medically necessary team comprises district staff, the student's parent/caregiver, parent-invited individuals, the school principal/administrator, general education teacher, assistant director of Integrated Services or Student Services, and other appropriate staff including IEP or Section 504 team members. A private health-care specialist is any person licensed, certified, or otherwise authorized to provide health-care services in Colorado, including pediatric behavioral health and autism services providers under specified statutes; district staff cannot qualify as private specialists under this policy.

NOTICE TO PARENTS/CAREGIVERS

Section 504 of the Rehabilitation Act and Title II of the Americans with Disabilities Act provide students access to medically necessary treatment essential for meaningful educational access or safe school attendance. Treatment not meeting these standards falls outside this policy's scope.

DETERMINATION PROCESS

Timeline and Initiation: parents submit requests using the district's form. The district schedules meetings as soon as reasonably practicable, and no later than 30 calendar days.

Team Responsibility: the medically necessary team determines whether treatment must occur in school settings to ensure educational access under Section 504 and ADA Title II.

Provider Participation: teams must invite the treating health-care specialist not less than ten calendar days in advance, with options for written submissions.

District Authority: the policy clarifies that districts may use qualified internal staff or contract providers rather than accepting third-party specialists, and nothing requires permitting third parties to determine special education services in ways conflicting with federal law obligations.

IEP/504 Coordination: following determination, the relevant team meets to discuss FAPE provision if necessary.

ACCESS TO SCHOOL PREMISES

Direct Treatment Access: private specialists may provide medically necessary treatment per team determination, subject to Policy KI (Visitors to Schools) and regulation JLCDC-R.

Observation and Collaboration: specialists may access school property to observe the student in the school setting or collaborate with school personnel, similarly subject to applicable policies. District staff may observe simultaneously.

Limitations: permission may be limited or revoked if the private health-care specialist violates this policy or District Regulation JLCDC-R, or demonstrates inability to follow requirements responsibly.

APPEAL PROCEDURE

When teams deny medically necessary treatment in schools, parents receive notice of appeal rights. Appeals must comply with these requirements: written notice to the assistant superintendent within ten school days; the District holds a hearing within ten school days of the appeal request; advance notice of hearing date, time, and location is provided; the hearing is conducted by a neutral individual without direct outcome interest; full opportunity to present relevant evidence is afforded; the right to representation at parent/student expense, including an attorney (with five school-day advance notice), applies; a written decision is issued within a reasonable timeframe; the decision is based solely on hearing evidence, including a summary and reasoning; and if the determination is overturned, the matter is remanded to the appropriate team with recommendations.

REPORTING REQUIREMENTS

Each school designates staff to regularly report to the superintendent: requesting student names, requests, and outcomes (accepted/denied).

LEGAL REFERENCES

42 U.S.C. §§ 1396, 1396d(r)(5) (Medicaid coverage requirement); C.R.S. 22-20-121 (medically necessary treatment in school settings); C.R.S. 25.5, articles 4, 5, 6 (state medical assistance); C.R.S. 10-16-104(1.4) (autism services); Section 504, Rehabilitation Act of 1973, 29 U.S.C. § 794; Title II, Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990.

CROSS REFERENCES

JLCD – Administering Medications to Students; JLCDB – Administration of Medical Marijuana to Qualified Students; JLCDC-R – Authorizing Private Health-Care Specialists; KI – Visitors in Schools.

ADOPTION/REVISION DATES

Adopted May 23, 2023 (effective July 1, 2023); revised June 9, 2026 (effective July 1, 2026).

## JLCDC-R — AUTHORIZING PRIVATE HEALTH-CARE SPECIALISTS FOR TREATMENT

This regulation implements Policy JLCDC and operates under section 504 of the Rehabilitation Act of 1973, 29 U.S.C. sec. 794, and Title II of the Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990.

INITIAL REQUIREMENTS BEFORE TREATMENT BEGINS

Written Treatment Plan: the district must prepare a plan identifying treatment form, location(s), and administration protocol with input from the specialist and parent/guardian. It must be signed by the administrator, student (if capable), specialist, and parent/guardian.

District Representative: the district must designate a representative with authority to schedule and cancel specialist visits.

Parental Consent: the student's parent signs a parental consent form to any medically necessary treatment in the school setting.

Confidentiality Affidavit: the specialist must commit to complying with the Family Educational Rights and Privacy Act (FERPA) and the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA).

Insurance Certificate: proof of General Liability, Auto Liability, and Professional Liability insurance meeting district standards is required.

Colorado Licensure: the private health-care specialist provides proof of Colorado licensure.

Assumption of Risk: the specialist waives claims for harm caused by the student or other students.

CONDITIONS DURING TREATMENT

Parties must avoid disruption to the learning environment and maintain instructional program integrity. The specialist must provide at least two weeks' advance notice for additional visits. The district retains discretion to deny, reschedule, or modify visits with two-week notice (except emergencies). The student's parent/guardian will be solely responsible for compensating the private health-care specialist. The specialist must follow all applicable state, federal, and district laws and policies. The district won't exercise supervisory control but may review services for coordination. Permission may be revoked if the specialist violates policy or fails to follow parameters.

ADOPTION DATE

Adopted by Superintendent: May 30, 2023, effective July 1, 2023.

## JLDAC — SCREENING/TESTING OF STUDENTS

This district policy establishes parental and student rights regarding surveys, assessments, and evaluations. It grants parents/caregivers and eligible students (age 18+ or emancipated minors) the right to review such materials, whether created internally or by third parties.

KEY PROTECTIONS REQUIRING CONSENT

Students cannot be required to participate in assessments revealing information about: political affiliations or beliefs of the student or student's family; mental/psychological conditions; sexual behavior and attitudes; illegal or self-incriminating behavior; critical appraisals of family members; privileged relationships (lawyers, clergy, physicians); income (except for program eligibility determination); religious practices or beliefs; and social security numbers.

The policy also prohibits collection and marketing of personal student information without prior written consent.

NOTICE REQUIREMENTS

Schools must provide written notice at least two weeks before administering such assessments, making documents available for review and offering information about records examined, examination methods, purposes, and access procedures.

EXCEPTIONS

Exceptions include journalism student work, mandatory child abuse reporting, health professional evaluations, suicide/threat assessments, and educational product/service recruitment activities.

PHYSICAL SCREENINGS
